  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_6fbfba5f-0a00-46c1-af7a-24e6425a89ab'
      });

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer10755888606049604929">Countless students, professors and general readers alike have relied upon The New Oxford Annotated Bible for essential scholarship and guidance to the world of the Bible. Now the Augmented Third Edition adds to the established reputation of this premier academic resource. A wealth of new maps, charts, and diagrams further clarify information found in the scripture pages. I</span>
  <span id="freeText10755888606049604929" style="display:none">Countless students, professors and general readers alike have relied upon The New Oxford Annotated Bible for essential scholarship and guidance to the world of the Bible. Now the Augmented Third Edition adds to the established reputation of this premier academic resource. A wealth of new maps, charts, and diagrams further clarify information found in the scripture pages. In addition, section introductions have been expanded and the book introductions have been made more uniform in order to enhance their utility. Of course, the Augmented Third Edition retains the features prized by students, including single column annotations at the foot of the pages, in-text background essays, charts, and maps, a page number-keyed index of all the study materials in the volume, and Oxford's renowned Bible maps. This timely edition maintains and extends the excellence the Annotated's users have come to expect, bringing still more insights, information, and approaches to bear upon the understanding of the biblical text.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10755888606049604929" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/45025306?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dylan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dylan" href="/user/show/1965112-dylan">Dylan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Dylan by: </span>
      Door to door salesman

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer45025306&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer45025306" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14194795381300869759">This book was terrible. The characters are two dimensional, the plot is all over the place and the author can't keep his story straight. Then halfway through they just introduce a new protagonist out of nowhere who dies within 4 chapters and they spend the rest of the book trying to work out what his deal was. <br/><br/>Genesis and Numbers are a huge yawn fest, I'm not even sure what the lengthy genealogies and census information had to do with the fisherman because everybody dies in the end anyway. <br/><br/>This </span>
  <span id="freeText14194795381300869759" style="display:none">This book was terrible. The characters are two dimensional, the plot is all over the place and the author can't keep his story straight. Then halfway through they just introduce a new protagonist out of nowhere who dies within 4 chapters and they spend the rest of the book trying to work out what his deal was. <br /><br />Genesis and Numbers are a huge yawn fest, I'm not even sure what the lengthy genealogies and census information had to do with the fisherman because everybody dies in the end anyway. <br /><br />This book is huge. Oh my god, it's soo long. I thought Lord of the Rings was big. The only consolation is that it's so big and heavy my wife sometimes uses it to press flowers, which is fine by me because I wouldn't lend this book to anyone. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14194795381300869759" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1337938129844802602">What did I NOT learn from this book?<br/><br/>This is the version of the Bible that I now use, and of the Bibles I have owned, it is the most useful. There are a number of essays at the beginning and end of the book, color maps, timelines, and all sorts of other information. Each chapter of the Bible is preceded by an introduction, placing the writing in a historical context. There are extensive footnotes on every page, explaining unfamiliar words and concepts, citing other scriptures where the ideas in t</span>
  <span id="freeText1337938129844802602" style="display:none">What did I NOT learn from this book?<br /><br />This is the version of the Bible that I now use, and of the Bibles I have owned, it is the most useful. There are a number of essays at the beginning and end of the book, color maps, timelines, and all sorts of other information. Each chapter of the Bible is preceded by an introduction, placing the writing in a historical context. There are extensive footnotes on every page, explaining unfamiliar words and concepts, citing other scriptures where the ideas in the current verse appear, and commenting upon the scripture itself. It is also handy to have the Apocrypha. I do a good bit of teaching, and, trust me, I am no Bible scholar. I simply don't think I could get up in front of a group of people and intelligently discuss the Bible if I did not have this book.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8805553727491274691">"A man hears what he wants to hear and disregards the rest." -- Paul Simon<br/><br/>Anyway . . . <br/><br/>This is what I like about The New Oxford Annotated Bible: <br/><br/>1. At the beginning of each book, you get a very detailed, literary, historical, and theological criticism. This is useful in understanding the context, which is imperative when reading The Bible appropriately. <br/><br/>2. The footnotes are incredible. It goes into explanation of word choices and translational issues. <br/><br/>3. Holds The Apocrypha, something I've never</span>
  <span id="freeText8805553727491274691" style="display:none">"A man hears what he wants to hear and disregards the rest." -- Paul Simon<br /><br />Anyway . . . <br /><br />This is what I like about The New Oxford Annotated Bible: <br /><br />1. At the beginning of each book, you get a very detailed, literary, historical, and theological criticism. This is useful in understanding the context, which is imperative when reading The Bible appropriately. <br /><br />2. The footnotes are incredible. It goes into explanation of word choices and translational issues. <br /><br />3. Holds The Apocrypha, something I've never studied before. <br /><br />This version of The Bible is great for anyone who is interested in the actual study of the book as opposed to just getting angry and saying stuff like "it's mysogynistic, homophobic, and obviously a work of fiction". Thumpers might want to stay away as well. You might not like your own version The Truth, either . . .</span>

        <a title="Jen/The Tolkien Gal/ジェニファー" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jen/The Tolkien Gal/ジェニファー" href="/user/show/4919283-jen-the-tolkien-gal">Jen/The Tolkien Gal/ジェニファー</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer13126151704884707978">I wanted to preface this by saying I'm going to be <b>reading the Bible and Qur'an parallel to each other for academic and philosophical reasons.</b> I've been interested in Abrahamic religions and want to start somewhere. <b>In no way am I doing this for religious reasons</b>, but purely because I want to understand theism. I was raised in a pseudo-Christian setting where on the surface level, everyone pretended to be Christian but really were undecided and ultimately didn't think. <br/><br/>I also don't think it's fa</span>
  <span id="freeText13126151704884707978" style="display:none">I wanted to preface this by saying I'm going to be <b>reading the Bible and Qur'an parallel to each other for academic and philosophical reasons.</b> I've been interested in Abrahamic religions and want to start somewhere. <b>In no way am I doing this for religious reasons</b>, but purely because I want to understand theism. I was raised in a pseudo-Christian setting where on the surface level, everyone pretended to be Christian but really were undecided and ultimately didn't think. <br /><br />I also don't think it's fair to read something that dissects principles from specific religions <b>before I am familiarised by the text itself.</b><br /><br /><b>I'm agnostic and way more spiritual</b> - in fact I'm more in tune with the spiritual ancestry of my Maori forefathers and believe in the interconnectedness of humanity. I'm incredibly tired of being shunned from discourse of theism due to Christian family saying I know nothing of the Bible and Qur-an - and thus cannot defend Islamic individuals or criticise Christian ones <b>in their eyes</b><br /><br /><b>After consulting Abrahamic religions I will move onto others.</b> I come from a country with a melting pot of religious beliefs, from ancestry worship, to Hinduism to Christianity etc. South Africa definitely benefits you in that way.<br /><br />I'm definitely open to people's thoughts - I'm more interested here in the <b>psychology and archetypes of religion than the content itself - I'm not here to indulge people who wish to convert me to anything, but I don't mind hearing your ideas on teachings at all.</b>. If you engage me on the basis of conversion I will not indulge you, but <b>whether you are religious or not and wish to discuss and challenge any of my thoughts, please feel free.</b></span>

<div id="review_534653549" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/534653549" />
    <a title="Benjamin Atkins" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7411747-benjamin-atkins"><img alt="Benjamin Atkins" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1359945989p2/7411747.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/534653549?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Benjamin Atkins" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Benjamin Atkins" href="/user/show/7411747-benjamin-atkins">Benjamin Atkins</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer534653549" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4612390658842941612">February 2012 I set out to read the Bible in one year. I felt that I had probably read the whole thing, almost certainly the New Testament, but with-out the context or continuity. I added an extra 3 months to add the Apocrypha. I started out using the NIV and the King James. After a couple of months I added this edition of the NRSV, after a few more months I was reading this version exclusively. I even purchased a second copy so that I could keep one at work and one at home making it easier to k</span>
  <span id="freeText4612390658842941612" style="display:none">February 2012 I set out to read the Bible in one year. I felt that I had probably read the whole thing, almost certainly the New Testament, but with-out the context or continuity. I added an extra 3 months to add the Apocrypha. I started out using the NIV and the King James. After a couple of months I added this edition of the NRSV, after a few more months I was reading this version exclusively. I even purchased a second copy so that I could keep one at work and one at home making it easier to keep up on the daily reading schedule.<br /><br />I have read some negative comments complaining of historical inaccuracies and continuity problems in this work. Talk about missing the point! The Bible Is truly a monumental achievement of literature (and to many of us) of spirituality. It begs to be appreciated on either level or even better on both. Other classical works of literature (Homer, Beowulf, Gilgamesh and The Arabian Nights amongst others), history (Herodotus, Thucydides, Eusebius, Plutarch all come mind), philosophy (Plato, Aristotle) and religion (Koran and Bhagavad-Gita) display similar inconsistencies yet we would be at best considered intellectually deficient to reject these works. At worst we would be seen as culturally insensitive. <br /><br />It seems that often in the Western tradition the Bible is held to a different standard. As with the other great works it was written by man. Never mind the argument that if it were divinely inspired it would be "perfect." If you you believe in divine inspiration, you can imagine rationalizations providing for God to leave it imperfect. If you don't believe, well, you get what you have.<br /><br />I heard someone say in my youth that even if the Bible is not the divine word of God, it is still the best guide going to living your life. While it may be difficult to see this is the Old Testament, it does serve to establish a context for the "new" message of the New Testament.<br /><br />What I liked: The historical essays leading off each book, section and Athens are simply outstanding. Many I read multiple times. Only word of warning here is that hey we obviously written by different authors. Occasionally they do conflict or offer repetitive information. Over-all, I found these essays critical in understanding what I was reading from both a historical and literary perspective. Likewise, the notes accompanying the text are thorough, enlightening and informative. Also I came away with a new appreciation for the wisdom and literature in some of the minor Old Testament books and even more so the Apocrypha.<br /><br />What I didn't like: The text itself was definitely more challenging than the NIV (though probably less than the King James). I am no expert on translating but I understand that NRSV is leans more towards the literal side of the continuum than the interpretive perhaps explaining the "thicker" text. I found that with the NRSV I needed to work harder on focusing my attention.<br /><br />Over-all it was a great experience and for me personally, a worthwhile endeavor. I would highly recommend if you haven't already undertaken a similar project that you consider doing so.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4612390658842941612" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1991356892" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1991356892" />
    <a title="Michael Walker" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58751084-michael-walker"><img alt="Michael Walker" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473189463p2/58751084.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1991356892?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Walker" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Walker" href="/user/show/58751084-michael-walker">Michael Walker</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780195283662" href="/book/show/689632.The_New_Oxford_Annotated_Bible_New_Revised_Standard_Version_with_the_Apocrypha">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58751084-michael-walker?shelf=bible">bible</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1991356892" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11387383251563716701">English Bible translations fall somewhere along a continuum between a woodenly literal rendition and a free, or liberal, style. An example of the former is the venerable King James Version with its flowery 17th-c. language, and the modern-sounding "The Message," as rendered by its translator, Eugene Peterson. The present work is an attempt to update the RSV. On the above-referenced continuum it falls between the KJV and the New International Version (NIV), the latter an attempt to strike a judic</span>
  <span id="freeText11387383251563716701" style="display:none">English Bible translations fall somewhere along a continuum between a woodenly literal rendition and a free, or liberal, style. An example of the former is the venerable King James Version with its flowery 17th-c. language, and the modern-sounding "The Message," as rendered by its translator, Eugene Peterson. The present work is an attempt to update the RSV. On the above-referenced continuum it falls between the KJV and the New International Version (NIV), the latter an attempt to strike a judicious balance between word-for-word literalism and a paraphrase. <br />The NRSV is annotated, supposedly for the college, or serious, Bible student. The notes are generally accurate, but tend to overly lean on current theological fads. One example is where the scholarly literary critical methods for determining the authors of the biblical books (Moses, David, Paul, etc.) trump the historical Church consensus. It appears this is decided solely on the basis of "scholarly weight" rather than actual facts - a sadly common fault in today's culture. Recommended, but with reservations.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11387383251563716701" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_441068870" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/441068870" />
    <a title="Marc" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4672541-marc"><img alt="Marc" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1424419713p2/4672541.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/441068870?book_show_action=true">Oct 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marc" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marc" href="/user/show/4672541-marc">Marc</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780195289565" href="/book/show/13158289-the-new-oxford-annotated-bible-with-apocrypha">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer441068870" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10740155488247140863">I've collected and discarded countless study Bibles over the past twenty years, from topical, devotional Bibles like the Life Application to hardcore scholarly Bibles like the NOAB 2nd Edition, and almost everything in between. A few years ago, I settled on the Catholic NAB for its rigorous--and surprisingly fair--notes and commentary, and for its valuable apocrypha. That Bible, however, suffers from a very slack, lackluster translation. <br/><br/>The 4th edition of the NOAB is, in my opinion, the very be</span>
  <span id="freeText10740155488247140863" style="display:none">I've collected and discarded countless study Bibles over the past twenty years, from topical, devotional Bibles like the Life Application to hardcore scholarly Bibles like the NOAB 2nd Edition, and almost everything in between. A few years ago, I settled on the Catholic NAB for its rigorous--and surprisingly fair--notes and commentary, and for its valuable apocrypha. That Bible, however, suffers from a very slack, lackluster translation. <br /><br />The 4th edition of the NOAB is, in my opinion, the very best study Bible available in English. The NRSV translation is excellent and literary. The notes and essays exhibit stupendous, fearless scholarship, without imposing itself on the biblical text (the NOAB team has wisely backed away from political correctness in favor of letting the Bible speak for itself). The theological commentary is as helpful as it can be without being biased. Rather than offering a single theological viewpoint, for example, different viewpoints of historical importance are offered, and the reader is allowed to choose which, if any, viewpoint seems most sensible. Again, the NOAB team lets the Bible speak for itself, from the times in which it was written, and help us read the Bible on its own terms, rather than imposing *our* terms upon it. <br /><br />This is perhaps the ultimate English study Bible for thinking persons and fearless believers. Although I treasure my old KJV and Rheimes-Douay, the NOAB 4th Edition is the best biblical investment I've ever made. If I'd had this Bible twenty years ago, I need never to have bought another.<br /><br />Note: I own the leather-bound, signature-bound "hardback" version of the 4th Edition NOAB (ISBN 978-0-19-528957-2). This Bible is simply too good for the regular, shoddily bound hardback or paperback formats. Unless you're a Bible-as-literature student who can only afford this book on the cheap, I urge you to invest in the high-quality leather, signature-bound NOAB. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10740155488247140863" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I have read about 2/3 of this version with intense study of the Old Testament (EFM)and find the annotation extremely helpful.   <span id="freeText1673371448068950945" style="display:none">Of all the versions of the Bible that I have read, I find this one the most helpful. I have read about 2/3 of this version with intense study of the Old Testament (EFM)and find the annotation extremely helpful. The more I learn about how the Bible was originally written and how changes and translations have been made over the decades, I find myself getting closer and closer to my understanding of truth. In particular, the references to original language are really revealing. Other versions of the Bible seem to have misinterpreted historic translations in favor of verse. All in all, I will keep reading in this Bible.</span>

  <span id="freeText8847089559349869171" style="display:none">I've read a good chunk of the Old Testament in the NRSV (picking up where I left off from Young's Literal Translation), and more or less the whole New Testament (might have switched back to KJV for an epistle or two), along with a smattering of the notes and commentary. <br /><br />I was wondering how much of a difference modern language, a few centuries of additional scholarship, etc. would make to the NRSV vs. the KJV. Honestly, from a holistic perspective, not that much. My preference tends towards KJV, largely for aesthetic reasons.</span>

<div id="review_2408355658" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2408355658" />
    <a title="Madeleine Lesieutre" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/62378287-madeleine-lesieutre"><img alt="Madeleine Lesieutre" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536533426p2/62378287.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2408355658?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Madeleine Lesieutre" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Madeleine Lesieutre" href="/user/show/62378287-madeleine-lesieutre">Madeleine Lesieutre</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/62378287-madeleine-lesieutre?shelf=read-summer-2018">read-summer-2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2408355658" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16665296270567772818">It’s funny it only occured to me that Leonard Cohen’s “Hallelujah” references King David as I was going through all of my bible notes, figuring out what to write for this review. David played the chord that pleased the Lord. David saw Bathsheba bathing on the roof. I can’t believe it took me this long to get that, but I never really thought about it before. <br/><br/>This book reminded me of books too. There was this one that I read when I was about ten. It was about a Jewish girl, and she was defending h</span>
  <span id="freeText16665296270567772818" style="display:none">It’s funny it only occured to me that Leonard Cohen’s “Hallelujah” references King David as I was going through all of my bible notes, figuring out what to write for this review. David played the chord that pleased the Lord. David saw Bathsheba bathing on the roof. I can’t believe it took me this long to get that, but I never really thought about it before. <br /><br />This book reminded me of books too. There was this one that I read when I was about ten. It was about a Jewish girl, and she was defending her Jewish identity, telling some kid about a Jewish queen who saved her people. I can’t quite put my finger on what the book was called, but the book of Esther so reminded me of it. Another book I’ve loved, Charlotte Bronte’s “Jane Eyre,” has a ton of biblical allusions (and now that I’ve read the Bible, I’d probably find more), and characters justifying their actions/ philosophies for life with the Bible, and other characters arguing against the actions/ philosophies for life that some characters justified with the Bible, with the Bible: <br /><br />There’s conflict and debate between Jane and St. John because of their differing interpretations of the Bible (and because St. John kind of just wants Jane to do what he wants her to do, and so he uses various passages in the Bible as an effort to coax her into doing what he wants), there’s Helen who uses the bible to preserve herself through her short and difficult life (she takes a “love them that hate you” approach, which Jane feels only lets people take advantage of Helen more than they already would), and there’s Mr. Brocklehurst who uses his interpretations of the Bible to justify treating the girls in his care so poorly. This all reminds me of Bigger’s argument against Christianity in Richard Wright’s book, “Native Son.” Bigger felt that Christianity was just another way of pushing marginalized people down. <br /><br />(Friedrich Nietzsche would have agreed with Bigger’s view, but in a sort of different way. Nietzsche had a huge problem with Christianity, because he thought it protected people from their envy --if they wanted sex, but didn’t have it, they could call it “purity,” or if they had to do the bidding of someone they hated, they could call it “obedience.” Nietzsche saw envy as a positive thing, motivating people to become what they really wanted to become.) <br /><br />In response to the character in Wright’s book, I would say: what about all of the historical efforts of the Catholic Church to relieve the poor (and their charity organizations that are still quite relevant today), or the Salvation Army, or what about how historically, a group in the Church of England were the first to protest against slave trade in England (Quakers played that role in the United States). But then again, people also used the Bible to justify slavery… there’s a verse in Colossians about slaves obeying their masters, but then all of Philemon is about how this one guy should embrace his old slave as a brother. <br /><br />It almost seems like you can justify/ refute anything with the Bible. I was already familiar with a lot of bible stories and a few different Christian traditions before I actually read the whole thing (I watched plenty of Veggie Tales as a little kid, went to a Catholic elementary school, did Quaker summer camp through middle school, and attended Lutheran and Episcopal services), but reading (and doing a little bit of research on historical context) gave me more insight on where different Christians are getting their justifications and refutations for various actions and philosophies for life. <br /><br />I have a friend who tried to teach a really casual class for younger kids at her church, but the parents of some boy were so outraged that she, as a female, was teaching a boy about their religion. Recently though, I attended an Episcopal service, and the priest was female. So, verses like 1 Timothy 2.8-14 -- “Let a woman learn in silence with all submissiveness. I permit no woman to teach or to have authority over men; she is to keep silent. For Adam was formed first, then Eve; and Adam was not deceived, but the woman was deceived and became a transgressor” -- obviously stood out to me. Some people think that this passage was for all women, and some think that it was only intended for women in Ephesus at the time, because they were taking part in teaching incorrect doctrines to the people in Ephesus. Also, according to various internet sources, “authority” might not be the most accurate translation of the word. It’s probably best if people learn Hebrew, Aramaic, Greek, and plenty about the first century before they start trying to interpret the Bible (especially as a code for how to live) by themselves! <br /><br />This was a tricky book. It took some perseverance at times (building instructions, chapters of genealogies, and repetitiveness in general got rather wearisome). At other times, it was a nostalgic endeavor for me (I got to read all of the Jesus stories I heard a hundred times as a little kid). Then there were times when I was totally weirded out (like when they cut up that concubine). Sometimes this book was pleasantly philosophical (as in Ecclesiastes, which was my favorite book to read). Other times, it was dark, and strange (as in Judges, and really in a lot of the historical books). I suppose those two qualities, together, make it a very human book. Now I’m thinking of the beginning of the first book of kings, how as David dies, he tells Solomon to be a good, and at the same time, tells him to go kill some people that have caused David trouble. It’s so human!</span>
  <a data-text-id="16665296270567772818" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer353991212" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7361562021573229964">The NRSV might be my new favorite translation. I also loved the background, at the beginning of each book, about authorship, naming, canonical status, structure, historical context, literary history and more! This is a great read from multiple angles. It is excellent literature, with a multitude of genres in an easy to read format. It is also, and in my opinion most importantly, the literary foundation of the Judeo-Christian faith.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6436166436989928442">This is the version I advise my students to get and to use.<br/><br/>The translation is, mostly, good. (Now and then I have a few quibbles, but no translation is going to be ultimately satisfying.) The notes are excellent, however, and set the texts in cultural contexts briefly but (again, usually) accurately. The editors are highly informed.<br/></span>

<div id="review_1403893859" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1403893859" />
    <a title="Amy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45667413-amy"><img alt="Amy" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1403893859?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amy" href="/user/show/45667413-amy">Amy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('cwLRQD2vx+buGT0CMd7x6abAphBwZ6OnCE/XON7S/UIadlMBmttkZ1FHg30e5X1+m/wbZRGqjoaGILo02XWjfQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('lKOAr6JxnI48JQaUVIdazReAoAzZz8xWHxSZXKOuH5j91wLuBQU/D4N7uOt7vNZaKrwdebgC4XeRe/RQpAlBpw==')})
  }

Borg<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.14 avg rating &mdash; 3,267 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2001\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer5238538209641612604\">Many Christians mistakenly believe that their only choice is either to reconcile themselves to a fundamentalist reading of scripture (a \"literal-factual\" approach) or to simply reject the Bible as something that could bring meaning and value into the<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText5238538209641612604\" style=\"display:none\">Many Christians mistakenly believe that their only choice is either to reconcile themselves to a fundamentalist reading of scripture (a &quot;literal-factual&quot; approach) or to simply reject the Bible as something that could bring meaning and value into their lives. In Reading the Bible Again for the First Time, Marcus Borg shows how instead we can freshly appreciate all the essential elements of the Old and New Testaments—from Genesis to Revelation—in a way that can open up a new world of intelligent faith.\n\nIn Reading the Bible Again for the First Time, Borg reveals how it is possible to reconcile a scientific and critical way of thinking with our deepest spiritual needs, leading to an insightful experience of ancient text. This unique book invites every reader—whatever his or her religious background—to engage the Bible, to wrestle with its meaning, to explore its mysteries, and to understand its relevance. //<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_13049091'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13049091-the-jewish-annotated-new-testament?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Jewish Annotated New Testament<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/126743.Amy_Jill_Levine?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Amy-Jill Levine<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.43 avg rating &mdash; 517 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2011\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer945170562068173994\">Although major New Testament figures--Jesus and Paul, Peter and James, Jesus\' mother Mary and Mary Magdalene--were Jews, living in a culture steeped in Jewish history, beliefs, and practices, there has never been an edition of the New Testament that <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText945170562068173994\" style=\"display:none\">Although major New Testament figures--Jesus and Paul, Peter and James, Jesus\' mother Mary and Mary Magdalene--were Jews, living in a culture steeped in Jewish history, beliefs, and practices, there has never been an edition of the New Testament that addresses its Jewish background and the culture from which it grew--until now. In The Jewish Annotated New Testament, eminent experts under the general editorship of Amy-Jill Levine and Marc Z. Brettler put these writings back into the context of their original authors and audiences. And they explain how these writings have affected the relations of Jews and Christians over the past two thousand years. An international team of scholars introduces and annotates the Gospels, Acts, Letters, and Revelation from Jewish perspectives, in the New Revised Standard Version translation. They show how Jewish practices and writings, particularly the Greek translation of the Hebrew Bible, influenced the New Testament writers. From this perspective, readers gain new insight into the New Testament\'s meaning and significance. In addition, thirty essays on historical and religious topics--Divine Beings, Jesus in Jewish thought, Parables and Midrash, Mysticism, Jewish Family Life, Messianic Movements, Dead Sea Scrolls, questions of the New Testament and anti-Judaism, and others--bring the Jewish context of the New Testament to the fore, enabling all readers to see these writings both in their original contexts and in the history of interpretation. For readers unfamiliar with Christian language and customs, there are explanations of such matters as the Eucharist, the significance of baptism, and &quot;original sin.&quot; For non-Jewish readers interested in the Jewish roots of Christianity and for Jewish readers who want a New Testament that neither proselytizes for Christianity nor denigrates Judaism, The Jewish Annotated New Testament is an essential volume that places these writings in a context that will enlighten students, professionals, and general readers.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"945170562068173994\" href=\"#\" First published in 1979 to critical acclaim, winning the National Book Award and the National Book Critics Circl<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText15039526949468057881\" style=\"display:none\">The Gnostic Gospels is a landmark study of the long-buried roots of Christianity, a work of luminous scholarship and wide popular appeal. First published in 1979 to critical acclaim, winning the National Book Award and the National Book Critics Circle Award, The Gnostic Gospels has continued to grow in reputation and influence over the past two decades. It is now widely recognized as one of the most brilliant and accessible histories of early Christian spirituality published in our time.\n\nIn 1945 an Egyptian peasant unearthed what proved to be the Gnostic Gospels, thirteen papyrus volumes that expounded a radically different view of the life and teachings of Jesus Christ from that of the New Testament. In this spellbinding book, renowned religious scholar Elaine Pagels elucidates the mysteries and meanings of these sacred texts both in the world of the first Christians and in the context of Christianity today.\n\nWith insight and passion, Pagels explores a remarkable range of recently discovered gospels, including the Gospel of Thomas and the Gospel of Mary Magdalene, to show how a variety of “Christianities” emerged at a time of extraordinary spiritual upheaval. Some Christians questioned the need for clergy and church doctrine, and taught that the divine could be discovered through spiritual search. Many others, like Buddhists and Hindus, sought enlightenment — and access to God — within. Such explorations raised questions: Was the resurrection to be understood symbolically and not literally? Was God to be envisioned only in masculine form, or feminine as well? Was martyrdom a necessary — or worthy — expression of faith? These early Christians dared to ask questions that orthodox Christians later suppressed — and their explorations led to profoundly different visions of Jesus and his message. \n\nBrilliant, provocative, and stunning in its implications, The Gnostic Gospels is a radical, eloquent reconsideration of the origins of the Christian faith.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"15039526949468057881\"
